Section Three

# Culture, Customs & Daily Life

Culture is the texture of your world — the unwritten rules everyone knows, the rituals that mark important moments, the things people do without thinking. It's how people greet each other, what they eat, what they believe about luck, how they raise their children, and what they do for fun.

> **A note before you dive in:** Not every question in this workbook will apply to your story, and that's perfectly fine. Skip what doesn't fit. If your world mirrors a real culture, focus on what's different — you don't need to invent every custom from scratch. Zero in on elements that create friction for your romance and details that deviate from what readers will assume.

---

## How Culture Works in Romantasy

Culture in romantasy functions as a constraint system — the social rules that determine what your characters can and can't do, what costs they pay for breaking the rules, and what hoops they must jump through to be together. The tighter the constraints, the higher the tension.

Think of your culture working in three ways for your romance:

**As an obstacle** — the rules your characters must navigate or break. Taboos against certain relationships, required courtship steps that can't be skipped, chaperoning that prevents privacy, reputation systems where being seen together causes damage, and family involvement that creates opposition.

**As an opportunity** — customs that create romantic moments. Festivals where normal rules relax, dances that allow sanctioned touch, gift-giving traditions that let characters express interest, ceremonies that force proximity, and traditions that create meaningful gestures.

**As revelation** — how characters interact with expectations shows who they are. Do they follow rules out of belief or fear? Do they break them impulsively or deliberately? The most interesting tension often comes from pairing one character who embodies cultural values with one who challenges them.

## Common Patterns: Culture in Romantasy

| Pattern | How It Works | What It Creates |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **The forbidden touch** | Culture restricts physical contact; every brush of hands becomes charged | Slow-burn tension; meaningful small gestures |
| **The reputation gamble** | Being seen together risks social ruin | Stakes for every private moment; secrets |
| **The courtship ladder** | Expected progression that can't be skipped | Frustration when feelings outpace propriety |
| **The chaperoned dance** | Must interact under watchful eyes | Stolen glances; coded communication |
| **The festival exception** | One night when rules relax | Pivotal scenes; masks and anonymity |
| **The cultural mistranslation** | Gesture means different things in different cultures | Misunderstanding; revelation when explained |

## Common Mistakes

**The Inconsistent Culture.** Cultural rules that apply when convenient and vanish when inconvenient make your stakes feel fake. Establish your rules clearly, enforce them consistently, and show real consequences when characters break them. If rules relax in certain circumstances, establish that pattern early.

**The Paper-Thin Taboo.** If something is supposedly forbidden but no one actually faces consequences, the tension evaporates. Make consequences concrete and serious — show other characters who've suffered them so readers know the threat is real.

**The Monolithic Culture.** When everyone in a society thinks exactly the same way with no internal variation, the world feels flat. Show variation: some people enforce rules strictly while others look the other way, older and younger generations differ, and your characters can find allies and enemies within their own culture.

---

## Daily Life & Routines

Before you can show how culture affects romance, you need to understand how people actually live. Romance happens *within* daily life, and understanding these patterns tells you when characters might cross paths and what "normal" looks like.

**How to write it:** Establish a baseline routine so readers feel the disruption when romance breaks it. A few details of daily habit — the tea always taken, the prayer always said — make it meaningful when your character is too distracted to complete them.

#### What's the rhythm of a typical day?

*When might your protagonist naturally cross paths with their love interest? What parts of the day offer opportunity for private moments?*

| Time | What Happens |
| --- | --- |
| Morning | Prayer at dawn; light breakfast; nobles receive petitioners while commoners begin work in fields or shops |
| Morning |  |
| Midday |  |
| Afternoon/Evening |  |
| Night |  |

#### What's the weekly or monthly rhythm?

*Market days? Rest days? Religious observances? Regular social gatherings?*

___

#### What do people do when they're not working?

*Leisure, entertainment, socializing — what fills non-work hours?*

___

## Home & Household

**How to write it:** Describe homes through what your POV character notices — their background determines what stands out. When a character enters their love interest's home for the first time, slow down and let specific objects reveal who this person is.

## Food & Meals

Food reveals culture. What people eat, how they prepare it, and who they share it with tells you everything about values, resources, and social bonds. You don't need to invent an entire cuisine — focus on the details that matter for your story.

#### What's the staple food in your world?

*Bread cultures feel different from rice cultures, which feel different from potato or maize cultures. What fills bellies?*

___

#### What's a typical meal for different classes?

*The gap between what nobles eat and what commoners eat reveals social divides your characters might cross.*

| Class | Typical Meal |
| --- | --- |
| Wealthy/noble | Multiple courses, meat daily, imported spices, wine — meals are social performances |
| Common/poor | Bread, pottage, vegetables in season, meat only on feast days — meals are fuel |
| Wealthy/noble |  |
| Middle/merchant |  |
| Common/poor |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |

#### How many meals per day, and when?

*When do people eat? Do they break fast at dawn? Is the main meal midday or evening? When might your characters share a table?*

___

#### Who prepares food, and who serves it?

*Servants? The women of the household? Professional cooks? Shared duties? Who's doing the work?*

___

#### What foods are considered luxuries or treats?

*Sugar, spices, exotic fruits, specific meats — what's special enough to gift or celebrate with?*

___

#### Are there foods that are forbidden, taboo, or restricted?

*Religious dietary laws? Class-based restrictions? Foods associated with certain groups?*

___

#### What drinks are common?

*Water, ale, wine, tea, coffee, milk — what do people drink daily vs. for celebration?*

___

#### What does sharing a meal mean?

*Is eating together a sign of trust? Friendship? Family bond? Business arrangement?*

___

#### What might your protagonist cook for or share with the love interest?

*Food can be intimate. What meal might become significant in your story?*

___

---

## Architecture & Building

How people build says a lot about who they are. A desert palace won't look anything like a northern fortress. Just think about what makes sense for your climate and culture. When your characters travel between regions, these differences help readers feel the change.

#### How Climate Shapes Architecture

*Consider how your world's climate affects how people build.*

| Climate | Architectural Adaptations |
| --- | --- |
| Hot/sunny | Thick walls, small windows, courtyards, flat roofs, light colors, shaded walkways, cross-ventilation |
| Rainy/humid | Steep roofs, covered walkways, raised foundations, gutter systems, overhangs, indoor-focused living |
| Cold/snowy | Thick insulation, steep roofs for snow, small windows, central hearths, vestibules/mudrooms |
| Windy/coastal | Low profiles, reinforced structures, windbreaks, secure shutters, anchored roofs |

#### What architectural style dominates your main setting?

*Research real-world architecture that inspires your world — note distinctive elements like columns, arches, domes, materials.*

___

#### What do different types of buildings look like?

*How can your protagonist tell a temple from a tavern at a glance?*

| Building Type | Distinctive Features |
| --- | --- |
| Palace/noble estate |  |
| Temple/place of worship |  |
| Merchant's home/shop |  |
| Common dwelling |  |
| Military/fortification |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |

## Superstitions & Folk Beliefs

Beyond formal religion, most cultures have folk beliefs — things everyone "knows" even if they can't explain why. These small details make your world feel lived-in.

### Reference: Common Superstitions & Omens

*Use this list for inspiration. Pick a few that fit your world's mood and culture.*

| Category | Examples |
| --- | --- |
| **Lucky/Unlucky Numbers** | Three for luck, seven for magic, thirteen cursed, odd numbers for the living, even for the dead |
| **Animal Omens** | Ravens mean death, white animals are blessed, black cats bring luck (or doom), owls warn of secrets |
| **Weather Signs** | Red sky warnings, thunder during weddings, rain on a journey's start, fog hiding spirits |
| **Protective Actions** | Iron wards off fae, salt purifies, crossing thresholds with right foot, spitting to avert evil eye |
| **Bad Luck Triggers** | Speaking a dead person's name, whistling at night, opening umbrellas indoors, breaking mirrors |
| **Good Luck Rituals** | Knocking wood, throwing coins in water, carrying charms, first-footing at new year, bride's "something old" |
| **Threshold Beliefs** | Don't step on cracks, carry brides over doorways, leave offerings at crossroads, dawn/dusk are dangerous |
| **Dreams & Visions** | Dreams of water mean change, falling dreams warn of pride, dreaming of someone means they're thinking of you |

#### What common superstitions exist?

*Lucky/unlucky numbers, colors, animals? Actions that invite bad fortune? Protective gestures?*

___

#### What everyday rituals do people perform without thinking?

*Knocking on wood, crossing fingers, small offerings, phrases spoken at certain moments:*

___

## Manners, Etiquette & Everyday Politeness

**How to write it:** Don't explain the rules — show the violation and its aftermath. Write the sharp intake of breath, the exchanged glance, the beat of silence. Small social errors often carry more romantic charge than big confrontations.

*What faux pas might an outsider commit? What rudeness could create tension or misunderstanding between your characters?*

## Core Values & Worldview

Every culture has foundational beliefs — the virtues people are taught from childhood, the qualities that earn respect, the failures that bring shame. Your characters exist within these value systems, shaped by them even when they rebel.

### Reference: Cultural Values & Traits

*Use this list when deciding what your culture values most. Pick 3-5 that define your world.*

| Category | Possible Values |
| --- | --- |
| **Personal Virtues** | Honor, loyalty, courage, humility, patience, wisdom, honesty, compassion, self-reliance, cunning |
| **Social Bonds** | Family duty, clan loyalty, hospitality, community harmony, respect for elders, friendship, romantic love |
| **Achievement** | Ambition, excellence, competition, innovation, artistic mastery, scholarly pursuit, martial prowess |
| **Spiritual** | Piety, ritual observance, connection to ancestors, mystical insight, fate acceptance, divine service |
| **Material** | Wealth, status, land ownership, beautiful possessions, comfort, security, legacy |
| **Freedom** | Independence, personal choice, adventure, wanderlust, self-expression, breaking tradition |
| **Order** | Tradition, hierarchy, obedience, propriety, law, duty, sacrifice for the greater good |
| **Power** | Strength, dominance, conquest, political influence, magical ability, control, leadership |

> *Which cultural values must your characters compromise or sacrifice to be together?*

#### What does your culture value most highly?

*Consider: honor, loyalty, strength, cunning, beauty, magical power, knowledge, piety, family, individual achievement, community, tradition, freedom, duty.*

| --- | --- |

| 2 |  |
| 3 |  |
| 4 |  |

## Taboos & Forbidden Things

What's forbidden reveals what a culture fears. In romantasy, taboos create your "forbidden love" stakes — and they work best when they're specific and consequential.

#### What actions, topics, or behaviors are taboo in your culture?

*Taboos reveal what a society fears. Which taboos will your characters dance around, and which will they ultimately cross?*

| Category | Taboo Behaviors |
| --- | --- |
| Physical (touch, proximity, eye contact) | Unmarried people can't be alone together; touching someone's hair is deeply intimate; sustained eye contact between strangers is aggressive |
| Physical (touch, proximity, eye contact) |  |
| Verbal (certain words, discussing feelings) |  |
| Social (cross-class interaction, being alone together) |  |
| Magical (certain powers, magical contact) |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |

#### What happens when someone breaks a taboo?

*Stakes need teeth. Make consequences real and specific so readers feel the danger when your characters risk transgression.*

| Severity | Consequence |
| --- | --- |
| Minor taboo broken |  |
| Moderate taboo broken |  |
| Major taboo broken |  |

## Reputation, Gossip & Public Opinion

In many societies, what people *believe* about you matters as much as what you actually do. Gossip spreads. Reputation is fragile. Once lost, it may never be recovered.

#### What destroys a reputation?

*What whispers could ruin your protagonist or love interest? How does their position make them vulnerable to gossip?*

| Position | What Destroys Their Reputation |
| --- | --- |
| Someone of the protagonist's position |  |
| Someone of the love interest's position |  |

#### Can a ruined reputation be recovered? How?

*Is redemption possible in your world, or are some falls permanent? This affects how much your characters risk for love.*

___

## Touch, Proximity & Physical Boundaries

In many cultures, who can touch whom — and how — is heavily regulated. Touch restrictions are powerful tools for slow-burn romance. When a brush of fingers is transgressive, it carries a charge that explicit contact in a permissive culture can't match.

#### What touch is considered appropriate between unmarried people?

*Touch restrictions make every brush of skin meaningful. Define the boundaries your characters will push against.*

| Category | What's Included |
| --- | --- |
| Always acceptable | Formal handshake when introduced; brief touch on arm to get attention; passing objects hand to hand |
| Borderline or scandalous | Hand-holding without gloves; dancing closer than arm's length; any touch lasting more than a moment |
| Always acceptable |  |
| Acceptable in certain contexts |  |
| Borderline or scandalous |  |
| Absolutely forbidden |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |

#### What touch is considered particularly intimate — perhaps more so than a kiss?

*Some cultures consider hand-holding more intimate than other acts. Hair-touching. Using someone's true name. Sustained eye contact. What's YOUR culture's unexpected intimacy?*

___

#### When do your characters first break the touch barrier?

*What boundary do they cross? Was it intentional or accidental? How do they react?*

___

## Dress, Appearance & Adornment

What people wear signals who they are — class, status, availability, beliefs. Clothing rules create romantic tension: the veil that can't be removed, the color that marks someone as spoken for, the hair that only a spouse may see unbound.

#### What does clothing communicate in your culture?

*What can your protagonist tell about someone at a glance? What might they wear to signal availability or disguise their identity?*

#### Historical Fashion Terms Reference

If your world draws on medieval or Renaissance styles, here are some common terms. Pick the ones that feel right for your setting — or ignore them entirely if your characters wear something different.

| Term | Description |
| --- | --- |
| Chemise/shift | Basic undergarment worn against skin; linen, usually white |
| Doublet | Fitted men's jacket, often padded, worn over shirt |
| Kirtle | Women's fitted dress worn over chemise, under outer gown |
| Breeches | Men's trousers, often knee-length |
| Stays/corset | Structured undergarment shaping the torso |
| Petticoat | Underskirt(s) for warmth and volume |
| Jerkin | Sleeveless jacket, often leather |
| Cloak/mantle | Outer wrap for warmth and protection |

## Courtship Customs & Romantic Expectations

Every culture has scripts for romance — expected ways to show interest, accepted progressions from stranger to spouse. Understanding what's "normal" makes deviation meaningful.

**How to write it:** The tension in courtship comes from the gap between what's expected and what's felt. When characters deviate from the script, make it clear both know what they're doing — that's where the charge lives.

#### How do people show romantic interest?

*Consider: gift-giving, specific flowers or tokens, formal requests through family, dances, competitions, magical displays, love letters, poetry, meaningful glances.*

___

#### What's the expected progression of a relationship?

*Map the steps from "strangers" to "married":*

| Step | What Happens |
| --- | --- |
| 1 | Formal introduction through families; suitor sends a white rose to indicate interest |
| 2 | Chaperoned conversations at public events; dancing permitted at balls |
| 1 |  |
| 2 |  |
| 3 |  |
| 4 |  |
| 5 |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |

#### What's the role of family in courtship?

*Must families approve? Arrange meetings? Give formal consent?*

___

---

## Tokens, Gifts & Symbols

Gift-giving in courtship is a language of its own. The right gift at the right time signals interest; the wrong gift signals presumption or ignorance.

**How to write it:** A gift scene has three beats: the giving, the receiving, and the aftermath. A gift that shows the giver paid attention lands harder than an expensive one that only shows wealth.

#### Are there traditional courtship gifts or tokens?

*What objects carry romantic meaning? (Flowers, jewelry, handkerchiefs, enchanted items, etc.)*

___

#### What does giving or accepting such a token signify?

*Interest? Serious intent? Engagement? Binding commitment?*

___

---

## Gender Roles & Expectations

Gender — or equivalent social categories in your world — shapes behavior, opportunities, and relationship dynamics. Gender expectations often determine who pursues and who is pursued, who can act and who must wait.

**How to write it:** Show characters bumping against expectations through accumulated small frictions, not just dramatic confrontations. When two characters with different relationships to gender roles fall for each other, let them see each other differently than the world does.

#### How do these expectations affect your characters?

*Do they conform, resist, or navigate around expectations? How does their relationship to gender roles shape their desires and fears?*

| Character | How Gender Expectations Affect Them |
| --- | --- |
| Protagonist |  |
| Love Interest |  |

#### Does either character violate gender expectations?

*How? What consequences do they face — social, legal, or personal?*

## Children & Education

How a society raises its children reveals what it values most. Your characters were shaped by how they were raised — and if they have or will have children, these questions become personal.

## Festivals, Holidays & Celebrations

Special occasions compress time, force proximity, and create heightened emotional moments. They're classic romance settings — the energy is different, rules may relax, and everyone is gathered.

## Social Hierarchy in Practice

Even without laws, social hierarchy creates rules. Everyone knows how to behave around their betters and their lessers.

---

## Language, Names & Forms of Address

How people speak to each other reveals and creates intimacy. The shift from formal to informal address can be as significant as a first kiss. Names carry power in many fantasy settings.

**How to write it:** Build anticipation for the name shift by establishing formality early. The moment one character uses the other's first name — who initiates it, whether it's asked or simply taken — can carry as much weight as a first kiss.

#### What different forms of address exist?

*The shift from formal to informal address can be as significant as a first kiss. What progression exists in your world?*

| Type | Examples in Your World |
| --- | --- |
| Formal (titles, honorifics, surnames) |  |
| Informal (first names, nicknames) |  |
| Intimate (pet names, private names) |  |

#### When do your characters shift how they address each other?

*This is often a pivotal intimacy moment:*

| Aspect | Details |
| --- | --- |
| From |  |
| To |  |
| When and why |  |

#### Is there significance to names in your world?

*True names with power? Secret names? Multiple names for different contexts?*

___

## Quick Reference: Cultural Stakes

| Element | Your World |
| --- | --- |
| Core cultural value that most affects your romance |  |
| Main taboo your characters break |  |
| The reputation risk |  |
| The touch or proximity rule that creates tension |  |
| The courtship custom they follow or violate |  |
| The festival or ceremony that matters |  |
| The form of address shift (from > to > when) |  |

> **Final thought:** Don't info-dump culture. Show it through action — characters following or breaking rules, social awkwardness when norms are violated. The best cultural worldbuilding is invisible until someone breaks the rules, and then everyone knows exactly what line was crossed.

---

Section Four

# Economy, Labor & Resources

Money makes the world go round — even fantasy worlds. Economics determines who has power and who serves, who can choose their life and who has no choice at all. It shapes what your characters do all day, what they aspire to, and what they're willing to sacrifice.

---

## How Economics Works in Romantasy

Economics in romantasy creates tension through three main forces:

**Imbalance** — when one character has significantly more wealth than the other. Power dynamics shift, generosity can feel like control, pride and shame enter the relationship, and the question "do they love me or my money?" haunts the wealthy.

**Pressure** — when financial necessity forces choices. Arranged marriages for money or alliance, accepting employment from a love interest, choosing security over love, debt creating obligation.

**Transaction** — when money or resources pass between lovers. Gifts carry meaning beyond their value, help can feel like charity, refusal can feel like rejection, and "I owe you" creates imbalance.

## Common Patterns: Economics in Romantasy

| Pattern | How It Works | What It Creates |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **The wealth gap** | One character rich, one poor | Power imbalance; questions about motives |
| **The economic rescue** | Wealthy character can solve poor character's problems | Gratitude vs. obligation; "am I being bought?" |
| **The arrangement** | Financial necessity forces proximity | Forced proximity; "is this real?" |
| **The debt** | One owes the other (money, life, service) | Obligation; when is the debt paid? |
| **The sacrifice** | One gives up wealth/security for love | High stakes; proving love isn't about money |
| **The inheritance threat** | Being together means losing fortune | External pressure; choosing love or security |

## Common Mistakes

**The Meaningless Wealth Gap.** One character is rich and one is poor, but it never affects anything. Make the gap matter — the poor character faces real scarcity, and the rich character's money tangibly affects the power dynamic between them.

**The Infinite Wealth.** The wealthy character can buy their way out of any problem, so the story loses tension. Give wealth limits: money tied up in family trusts, controlled by others, or simply unable to solve this particular problem.

**The Invisible Economy.** Characters exist in an economic vacuum where no one works or worries about money. Ground your characters in economic reality — readers should understand how they survive, and choices should have financial consequences.

---

## Economic Systems

#### What type of economy exists in your world?

*Consider how the economic system shapes daily life and what opportunities exist for your characters. Does it allow for social mobility, or are people locked into their station?*

| Type | Check | How Wealth Works |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Feudal/Agrarian |  | Land-based wealth; peasants work land owned by lords |
| Mercantile/Trade |  | Wealth from commerce; merchant class rising |
| Guild-Based |  | Professions controlled by guilds; must apprentice |
| Magical Economy |  | Magic is primary source of wealth/production |
| Scarcity/Survival |  | Resources desperately scarce; survival is primary concern |

#### How is wealth created in your world?

*Agriculture? Trade? Craftsmanship? Mining? Magic? Conquest?*

___

#### How does wealth move?

*Can it be earned through work? Only inherited? Granted by rulers? Won through conquest?*

___

---

## Currency & Scale

#### What's the rough scale of money?

*Readers need to feel the weight of money in your story. What costs would make your protagonist wince, and what would your love interest spend without thinking?*

| Item | Approximate Cost |
| --- | --- |
| Day's unskilled labor wage |  |
| Simple meal |  |
| Night at an inn |  |
| A decent horse |  |
| A year's comfortable living |  |
| Day's unskilled labor wage | 5 copper pieces; a skilled craftsman earns 2 silver |
| Simple meal | 2 copper at a tavern; 1 copper from a street vendor |
|  |  |
|  |  |

### Currency Design

You don't need to design a complete monetary system, but a few details about currency can make your world feel lived-in. What's stamped on a coin tells readers who's in power. What materials are used tells them what's valued.

#### What materials is your currency made from?

*Gold, silver, copper? Magical crystals? Stamped leather? Carved bone? The material itself says something about your world's resources and values.*

___

#### What's depicted on your currency?

*Monarchs' faces? Religious symbols? Mythical creatures? National emblems? Consider what the ruling powers want citizens to see every time they make a transaction.*

___

#### What are your currency denominations called?

*Crowns, sovereigns, dragons, marks, florins? Names often reflect history, rulers, or cultural values. A coin called a "blessing" implies different values than one called a "skull."*

___

#### How do denominations relate to each other?

*10 coppers to a silver? 20 silvers to a gold? Unusual ratios (like 12 or 7) can make your world feel distinct.*

___

#### Design Your Currency

*Use this table to detail your currency system.*

| Denomination | Material | Value | What's Depicted |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Crown | Gold | 100 coppers | Reigning monarch on front, royal crest on back |
| Talon | Silver | 10 coppers | Griffin clutching a sword |
| Bit | Copper | Base unit | Sheaf of wheat |
|  |  |  |  |
|  |  |  |  |
|  |  |  |  |
|  |  |  |  |

## Work & Occupations

### Reference: Fantasy Professions & Jobs

*Use this list for inspiration when filling in occupation tables. What work shapes your characters' daily lives?*

| Category | Examples |
| --- | --- |
| **Nobility & Governance** | Lord/lady, steward, seneschal, ambassador, tax collector, herald, court advisor, magistrate |
| **Military & Combat** | Knight, soldier, guard, mercenary, assassin, archer, cavalry, siege engineer, weapons master |
| **Skilled Trades** | Blacksmith, carpenter, mason, tailor, cobbler, jeweler, glassblower, tanner, cooper, wheelwright |
| **Merchants & Trade** | Shopkeeper, trader, caravan master, ship captain, money-lender, auctioneer, innkeeper, tavern owner |
| **Service & Household** | Servant, maid, butler, cook, groom, lady's maid, valet, housekeeper, gardener, steward |
| **Food & Agriculture** | Farmer, shepherd, miller, baker, butcher, brewer, vintner, fisherman, beekeeper, hunter |
| **Healing & Medicine** | Healer, midwife, herbalist, apothecary, physician, surgeon, plague doctor, dentist |
| **Religious & Spiritual** | Priest, priestess, monk, nun, temple keeper, oracle, shrine maiden, inquisitor, exorcist |
| **Magical Professions** | Court mage, hedge witch, enchanter, potion-maker, diviner, ward-smith, spell-scribe, familiar handler |
| **Arts & Entertainment** | Bard, musician, actor, painter, sculptor, storyteller, jester, acrobat, dancer, playwright |
| **Scholarly & Learned** | Scribe, scholar, tutor, librarian, historian, cartographer, astronomer, alchemist, translator |
| **Underworld & Shadows** | Thief, pickpocket, smuggler, fence, spy, informant, bounty hunter, poisoner, tomb robber |

**How to write it:** Work leaves marks — callused hands, ink-stained fingers, the way someone stands. There's intimacy in watching someone do what they're good at, and in being seen doing it by someone who appreciates your skill.

#### What professions are respected? Looked down upon?

*Occupational status shapes how people are treated and what doors open or close. If your characters have different occupations, how might society view their pairing?*

| Status | Occupations |
| --- | --- |
| Highly respected | Court mages, master healers, high priests, landed nobility |
| Disreputable or shameful | Hedge witches, fortune-tellers, gravediggers, money-lenders |
| Highly respected |  |
| Respectable/ordinary |  |
| Necessary but low-status |  |
| Disreputable or shameful |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |

*Show the gap through concrete details. What does each character's home look like? What do they eat? What small luxuries does one take for granted that the other dreams of?*

### Natural Resources and Culture

What a place has shapes who its people become. You don't need to catalog every resource — just think about the one or two things that define your main setting and create the backdrop for your characters' daily lives.

#### How do your world's primary resources shape local cultures?

*Consider how abundance or scarcity of certain resources creates distinct traditions, crafts, and ways of life.*

| Resource | How It Shapes Culture |
| --- | --- |
| Timber | Carpentry valued, forests sacred, wooden architecture dominates |
| Gold/Precious metals | Mining economy, ornate craftsmanship, wealth displays define status |
| Wool/Textiles | Weaving traditions passed down, nomadic herders, vibrant textile markets |
| Grain/Agriculture | Harvest festivals central to calendar, farming cycles dictate life, land ownership matters deeply |
| Salt | Preservation culture, salt mining communities, unique cuisine and trade routes |
| Fish/Sea | Coastal culture, sailing traditions, sea worship, ports as cultural hubs |

## The Wealth Gap Dynamic

If your characters have different economic positions, that gap runs deeper than "one has money, one doesn't." The most interesting wealth-gap romances don't resolve this by pretending money doesn't matter — they work through it.

**How to write it:** Show the gap through small moments: one character casually ordering what the other couldn't afford in a month. Don't resolve it quickly — let characters struggle with pride, obligation, and the way money complicates everything.

#### What can the wealthier character provide that the other can't access alone?

*Safety? Comfort? Opportunity? Legal protection? Social access?*

___

#### What does the less wealthy character bring that money can't buy?

*Skills? Knowledge? Magical ability? Street smarts? Moral clarity?*

___

#### How does the wealth gap affect their power dynamic?

*Does one depend on the other? Is there resentment? Does the wealthy one use money as control?*

___

#### How do they each feel about the gap?

*Shame? Guilt? Obliviousness? Resentment? These feelings may never be spoken aloud, but they shape every interaction.*

| Character | How They Feel About the Gap |
| --- | --- |
| Wealthier character |  |
| Less wealthy character |  |

---

## Money, Gifts & What They Mean

When money or expensive gifts pass between people who care about each other, it's never simple. For the giver, a gift can be an expression of care — or a substitute for harder things. For the receiver, it can feel like love made tangible — or like charity.

**How to write it:** Slow down when one character offers financial help and the other must respond. Show the internal calculation — weighing need against pride, wondering what accepting means, noticing how the words are chosen.

#### If the wealthier character offers to pay for something, how does the other feel?

*Grateful? Humiliated? Obligated? Suspicious of motives?*

___

#### What financial gesture would be genuinely romantic — not transactional?

*Not buying something expensive, but noticing what they need and providing it without being asked. The gesture that says "I see you" rather than "I can afford this."*

___

---

## Debt, Obligation & Economic Coercion

Debt creates a relationship before there is a relationship. The problem with debt in romance is that it clouds consent.

#### Is either character in debt?

*Debt creates obligation, and obligation clouds consent. Does debt to the love interest (or a third party) complicate the romance?*

| Aspect | Details |
| --- | --- |
| To whom |  |
| For what (money, life saved, magical service) |  |
| What happens if they can't repay |  |

---

## Labor & Occupation in Romance

What people do for work isn't just how they earn money — it's how they spend most of their waking hours, what skills they develop, and often how they see themselves.

#### What does your protagonist do to survive?

*Work shapes who we become. What skills, knowledge, and worldview has their occupation given them?*

| Aspect | Details |
| --- | --- |
| Occupation |  |
| Skills learned through work |  |

#### What does your love interest do to survive?

*How does their work connect to or conflict with your protagonist's world? Do their occupations create proximity or distance?*

| Aspect | Details |
| --- | --- |
| Occupation |  |
| Skills learned through work |  |

#### How do their occupations bring them together or keep them apart?

*Same workplace? Completely separate worlds? Does one employ the other?*

___

---

## Economic Sacrifice

One of romance's most powerful beats is the moment a character gives up material security for love. Economic sacrifice proves love is real in a way words cannot.

**How to write it:** Make the sacrifice concrete: the smaller apartment, the mended clothes, the dinner that's mostly bread. Economic sacrifice is most powerful when it's ongoing — choosing every day to live with less because what you gained matters more.

#### What economic security does each character sacrifice to be together?

*What inheritance, income, protection, or opportunity do they lose by choosing each other? The sacrifice should cost enough to prove the love is real.*

| Character | What They Sacrifice |
| --- | --- |
| Protagonist |  |
| Love Interest |  |

---

## Quick Reference: Economic Stakes

| Element | Your World |
| --- | --- |
| Type of economy |  |
| Primary economic obstacle for romance |  |
| Economic power imbalance |  |
| The valuable/scarce resource |  |
| Economic debt or obligation |  |
| Economic sacrifice required to be together |  |

> **Final thought:** The question isn't "how does the tax system work?" — it's "what would this character do if they couldn't eat tomorrow?" Economics creates stakes when it forces impossible choices: love or survival, pride or security. Build an economy that makes these choices real.

Section Five

# Government, Power & Law

*Politics, law, forbidden relationships, enemies-to-lovers*

Who holds power in your world, how did they get it, and what laws keep your characters in line? This section helps you build the political structures, laws, and conflict that shape your characters' choices — and shows how political realities create romantic tension, from forbidden unions to enemies-to-lovers.

Not every question here will apply to your story — skip what doesn't fit. If politics is background in your world, keep it simple and develop just enough to make things feel real.

---

## Types of Government Reference

You don't need to invent a government from scratch. Most fantasy worlds draw on familiar types, and that's a strength, not a weakness.

Here's a quick reference to get you started. Think about which one fits your world — or mix and match. The right choice depends on what conflicts and constraints you want your characters to navigate.

| Type | Description | Romantic Implications |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Monarchy** | King/Queen rules, power by bloodline | Succession drama, forbidden love across ranks |
| **Empire** | Multiple states under one emperor | Characters from different conquered regions |
| **Oligarchy** | Small group of wealthy/powerful rule | Family alliances, marriage as power play |
| **Republic** | Elected representatives make laws | Political marriages, faction rivalries |
| **Theocracy** | Religious leaders hold power | Forbidden love, duty vs desire |
| **Federation** | States united under central government | Regional loyalties, border tensions |

#### Which government type(s) does your world use?

*Your world might use one type, combine elements from several, or have different systems in different regions.*

___

## Forms of Government

Different political structures create different pressures, different power dynamics, and different possibilities for your characters.

### Government Types

#### What form of government exists?

*The form of government determines who has power to make your characters' lives easier or harder, and what paths exist to change unjust laws.*

| Type | How Power Works | Common Features |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Absolute Monarchy** | Single ruler with unchecked power | Succession crises, court intrigue, ruler's whim is law |
| **Constitutional Monarchy** | Ruler limited by laws/councils | Balance of power, competing factions, reform possible |
| **Feudal System** | Hierarchy of lords owing loyalty upward | Local power varies, divided loyalties, regional differences |
| **Theocracy** | Religious authority rules | Divine law, religious hierarchy holds power, heresy is treason |
| **Magocracy** | Magical power determines rule | Non-mages marginalized, magical bloodlines matter |
| **Oligarchy** | Small group holds power (nobles, merchants, mages) | Factional conflict, closed system, hard to break in |
| **Republic/Democracy** | Elected or representative rule | Popular opinion matters, elections as pressure points |
| **Empire** | Central power over conquered territories | Core vs. periphery, colonial dynamics, rebellion threats |
| **Tribal/Clan** | Power through kinship groups | Family loyalty paramount, inter-clan politics |
| **Council Rule** | Decisions by committee | Consensus needed, gridlock possible, coalition building |

#### Which describes your world?

*You can combine elements or note regional differences. Consider how this system creates obstacles for your romance.*

___

#### If your world has multiple nations, do they have different systems?

*Political differences between nations can separate lovers or offer escape routes. Where would your characters flee if they had to?*

___

### Current Rulers

#### Who currently holds power?

*This person (or council) controls your characters' fate. Do they have reason to help or hinder the romance?*

| Aspect | Details | Example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Title/Position | | *High Queen of the Thornwood Realm* |
| Name | | *Queen Isaveth Dawnmere — feared and revered in equal measure* |
| How they gained power | | *Inherited through the sacred bloodline after her mother's mysterious death; rumors persist of darker means* |
| How long they've ruled | | *Twelve years — long enough to consolidate power, short enough that rivals still remember alternatives* |
| | | |
| | | |

#### What do they want?

*What are the current ruler's goals, fears, and priorities?*

___

#### What threatens their rule?

*Every power structure has vulnerabilities:*

___

## How Laws Work

Understanding how laws are made, changed, and enforced tells you what obstacles are truly immovable and which might bend.

#### Who makes laws?

*Ruler by decree? Legislative body? Religious authority? Tradition that no one changes? Magical compulsion?*

___

#### Can laws be changed? How?

| Method | How It Works | How Difficult |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Ruler's decree | | |
| Legislative process | | |
| Religious reinterpretation | | |
| Revolution/conquest | | |
| Legal challenge/appeal | | |
| Royal decree | The monarch can issue new edicts at will; nobles may petition for reversal | Easy for small matters; controversial changes invite rebellion |
| Ancient compact | Laws bound by magic at the realm's founding; breaking them has catastrophic consequences | Considered impossible until someone discovers a loophole |
| | | |
| | | |

#### Are there laws considered unchangeable?

*Divine law, ancient compacts, magically binding treaties — things that cannot be altered:*

| --- | --- | --- |
| The Queen's Shadow | Political crimes, treason, threats to the crown | Reports directly to the Queen; answers to no one else |
| City Watch | Common crimes, public order, market disputes | Local lord or city council; quality varies wildly by district |

| Aspect | Details |

| Do the accused have rights? | |
| Can verdicts be appealed? | |

## Political Structure & Hierarchy

> **Tip:** Show hierarchy through behavior, not explanation — who waits for whom, who speaks first, who sits while others stand. Romantic tension sparks in moments when the hierarchy falters: the powerful one asks instead of commands, the lower one refuses to defer.

## Magic & Political Power

If magic exists, it interacts with political power. Who has magic affects who rules.

#### How does magic relate to political authority?

*Magic can reinforce or threaten power structures. If one of your characters has magic, how does that affect their political standing?*

| Dynamic | How It Works |
| --- | --- |
| Mages rule (magocracy) | Political power comes from magical power |
| Mages serve | Government employs/conscripts mages |
| Mages are controlled | Laws restrict magic; mages are tracked/limited |
| Mages are separate | Magical and political authority are different spheres |
| Mages are persecuted | Magic is forbidden; mages are hunted |

#### Which describes your world?

*Consider how this relationship between magic and power affects your characters' standing and options.*

___

#### Who controls access to magical power?

*Gatekeepers create obstacles. Could your characters be denied magic, or forced to use it against their will?*

| Aspect | Details |
| --- | --- |
| How is magic acquired (birth, training, divine gift, artifacts)? | |
| Who controls training/access? | |
| Can magical power be taken away? | |

#### What laws govern magic use?

*What's forbidden? What's required? What's regulated?*

___

## Factions & Political Groups

Most political systems have competing factions — groups with different goals who struggle for power and influence.

| --- | --- | --- | --- |

#### Is there current factional conflict that affects your story?
